<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cost">Cost</span></h2>
<p>Pollution has a cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Manufacturing activities that cause <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a> impose health and clean-up costs on the whole of society. A manufacturing activity that causes air pollution is an example of a negative <a href="/wiki/Externality" title="Externality">externality</a> in production. A negative externality in production occurs "when a firm’s production reduces the well-being of others who are not compensated by the firm."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup> For example, if a laundry firm exists near a polluting steel manufacturing firm, there will be increased costs for the laundry firm because of the dirt and smoke produced by the steel manufacturing firm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> If external costs exist, such as those created by pollution, the manufacturer will choose to produce more of the product than would be produced if the manufacturer were required to pay all associated environmental costs. Because responsibility or consequence for self-directed action lies partly outside the self, an element of <a href="/wiki/Cost_externalization" title="Cost externalization">externalization</a> is involved. If there are external benefits, such as in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_safety" title="Public safety">public safety</a>, less of the good may be produced than would be the case if the producer were to receive payment for the external benefits to others. Goods and services that involve negative externalities in production, such as those that produce pollution, tend to be overproduced and underpriced since the externality is not being priced into the market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pollution can also create costs for the firms producing the pollution. Sometimes firms choose, or are forced by regulation, to reduce the amount of pollution that they are producing. The associated costs of doing this are called abatement costs, or <a href="/wiki/Marginal_abatement_cost" title="Marginal abatement cost">marginal abatement costs</a> if measured by each additional unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> In 2005 pollution abatement capital expenditures and operating costs in the US amounted to nearly $27 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dirtiest_industries">Dirtiest industries</span></h2>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blacksmith_Institute" title="Blacksmith Institute">Pure Earth</a>, an international non-for-profit organization dedicated to eliminating life-threatening pollution in the developing world, issues an annual list of some of the world's most polluting industries. Below is the list for 2016:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Lead%E2%80%93acid_battery" title="Lead–acid battery">Lead–acid battery</a> recycling</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">Mining</a> and <a href="/wiki/Extractive_metallurgy" title="Extractive metallurgy">extractive metallurgy</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lead_smelting" title="Lead smelting">Lead smelting</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tanning_(leather)" title="Tanning (leather)">Tanning</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Artisanal_mining" title="Artisanal mining">Artisanal mining</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">Landfills</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Industrial_park" title="Industrial park">Industrial parks</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chemical_industry" title="Chemical industry">Chemical industry</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">Manufacturing</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dyeing" title="Dyeing">Dyeing</a></li></ul>
<p>A 2018 report by the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Agriculture_and_Trade_Policy" title="Institute for Agriculture and Trade Policy">Institute for Agriculture and Trade Policy</a> and <a href="/wiki/GRAIN" title="GRAIN">GRAIN</a> says that the meat and dairy industries are poised to surpass the oil industry as the world's worst polluters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Textile_industry">Textile industry</span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Chemical_waste#Textile_industry" title="Chemical waste">Chemical waste § Textile industry</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Chemical_waste&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indigo_pollutionjpg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1535" data-file-width="2046" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Indigo_pollutionjpg.jpg/220px-Indigo_pollutionjpg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Indigo_pollutionjpg.jpg/330px-Indigo_pollutionjpg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Indigo_pollutionjpg.jpg/440px-Indigo_pollutionjpg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indigo_pollutionjpg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Indigo color water pollution in Phnom Penh, Cambodia, 2005</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Textile_industry" title="Textile industry">textile industry</a> is one of the largest polluters in the <a href="/wiki/Economic_globalization" title="Economic globalization">globalized</a> world of mostly free market dominated socioeconomic systems. Chemically polluted textile wastewater degrades the quality of the <a href="/wiki/Soil_conservation" title="Soil conservation">soil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The pollution comes from the type of conduct of chemical treatments used e.g., <a href="/wiki/Textile_manufacturing" title="Textile manufacturing">in pretreatment, dyeing, printing, and finishing operations</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> that many or most market-driven companies use despite "eco-friendly alternatives". Textile industry wastewater (TIWW) is considered to be one the largest polluters of water and soil <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, causing e.g., "carcinogenic, mutagenic, genotoxic, cytotoxic and allergenic threats to living organisms".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The textile industry uses over 8000 chemicals in its supply chain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> also pollutes the environment with large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Microplastics" title="Microplastics">microplastics</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> and has been identified in one review as the biggest pollution causing production sector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>A campaign of big clothing brands like Nike, Adidas and Puma to voluntarily reform <a href="/wiki/Textile_manufacturing" title="Textile manufacturing">their manufacturing</a> <a href="/wiki/Supply_chain_management" title="Supply chain management">supply chains</a> to commit to achieve zero discharges of hazardous chemicals by 2020 (global goal)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chemical_waste_destin_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chemical_waste_destin-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> appears to have failed.
</p>
Textile industry also creates a lot of pollution that leads to <a href="/wiki/Externality" title="Externality">externalities</a> which causes big problems in the economy. The problem usually occurs when there is no division of ownership rights. This means that problem of pollution is mainly caused because of lacking information about which company pollutes and at what scale the damage was caused by the pollution.</div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_fuel_related_industries">Fossil fuel related industries</span></h3>
<p>Outdoor air pollution attributable to fossil fuel use alone causes ~3.61 million deaths annually, making it one <a href="/wiki/List_of_causes_of_death_by_rate" title="List of causes of death by rate">of the top contributors to human death</a>, beyond being a major driver of climate change whereby greenhouse gases are considered per se as a form of pollution <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033199720">.mw-parser-output div.crossreference{padding-left:0}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable crossreference selfref" role="note">(see <a href="#Greenhouse_gas_emissions">above</a>)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS2019_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS2019-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Socially_optimal_level">Socially optimal level</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Right_to_a_healthy_environment" title="Right to a healthy environment">Right to a healthy environment</a></div>
<p>Society derives some indirect <a href="/wiki/Utility" title="Utility">utility</a> from pollution; otherwise, there would be no incentive to pollute. This utility may come from the consumption of goods and services that inherently create pollution (albeit the level can vary) or lower prices or lower required efforts (or inconvenience) to abandon or substitute these goods and services. Therefore, it is important that policymakers attempt to balance these indirect benefits with the costs of pollution in order to achieve an efficient outcome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs additional references to reliable sources. (July 2022)">additional citation(s) needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pollution-_A_Negative_Externality_in_Production.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="515" data-file-width="805" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Pollution-_A_Negative_Externality_in_Production.png/400px-Pollution-_A_Negative_Externality_in_Production.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Pollution-_A_Negative_Externality_in_Production.png/600px-Pollution-_A_Negative_Externality_in_Production.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Pollution-_A_Negative_Externality_in_Production.png/800px-Pollution-_A_Negative_Externality_in_Production.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pollution-_A_Negative_Externality_in_Production.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A visual comparison of the free market and socially optimal outcomes</div></div></div>
<p>It is possible to use <a href="/wiki/Environmental_economics" title="Environmental economics">environmental economics</a> to determine which level of pollution is deemed the social optimum. For economists, pollution is an "external cost and occurs only when one or more individuals suffer a loss of welfare". There is a socially optimal level of pollution at which <a href="/wiki/Welfare" title="Welfare">welfare</a> is maximized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> This is because consumers derive utility from the good or service manufactured, which will outweigh the <a href="/wiki/Social_cost" title="Social cost">social cost</a> of pollution until a certain point. At this point the damage of one extra unit of pollution to society, the <a href="/wiki/Marginal_cost" title="Marginal cost">marginal cost</a> of pollution, is exactly equal to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marginal_benefit" title="Marginal benefit">marginal benefit</a> of consuming one more unit of the good or service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Moreover, the feasibility of pollution reduction rates could also be a factor of calculating optimal levels. While a study puts the global mean loss of life expectancy (LLE; similar to <a href="/wiki/Years_of_potential_life_lost" title="Years of potential life lost">YPLL</a>) from air pollution in 2015 at 2.9 years (substantially more than, for example, 0.3 years from all forms of direct violence), it also indicated that a significant fraction of the LLE is unavoidable in terms of current economical-technological feasibility such as aeolian dust and wildfire emission control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1093/cvr/cvaa025_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1093/cvr/cvaa025-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>In markets with pollution, or other negative externalities in production, the <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free market</a> equilibrium will not account for the costs of pollution on society<b>.</b> If the social costs of pollution are higher than the private costs incurred by the firm, then the true supply curve will be higher. The point at which the social marginal cost and market <a href="/wiki/Demand" title="Demand">demand</a> intersect gives the socially optimal level of pollution. At this point, the quantity will be lower and the price will be higher in comparison to the free market equilibrium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-81">[81]</a></sup> Therefore, the free market outcome could be considered a <a href="/wiki/Market_failure" title="Market failure">market failure</a> because it "does not maximize efficiency".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>This model can be used as a basis to evaluate different methods of internalizing the externality. Some examples include <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">tariffs</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">carbon tax</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emissions_trading" title="Emissions trading">cap and trade</a> systems.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_environmental_pollution" title="History of environmental pollution">History of environmental pollution</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prior_to_19th_century">Prior to 19th century</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">Air pollution</a> has always accompanied civilizations. Pollution started from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric_times" title="Prehistoric times">prehistoric times</a>, when man created the first <a href="/wiki/Fire" title="Fire">fires</a>. According to a 1983 article in the journal <i>Science,</i> "<a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">soot</a>" found on ceilings of prehistoric caves provides ample evidence of the high levels of pollution that was associated with inadequate ventilation of open fires."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Metal <a href="/wiki/Forging" title="Forging">forging</a> appears to be a key turning point in the creation of significant air pollution levels outside the home. Core samples of <a href="/wiki/List_of_glaciers_in_Greenland" title="List of glaciers in Greenland">glaciers in Greenland</a> indicate increases in pollution associated with Greek, Roman, and Chinese metal production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DARK_CLOUDS_OF_FACTORY_SMOKE_OBSCURE_CLARK_AVENUE_BRIDGE_-_NARA_-_550179.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2919" data-file-width="1911" decoding="async" height="336" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/DARK_CLOUDS_OF_FACTORY_SMOKE_OBSCURE_CLARK_AVENUE_BRIDGE_-_NARA_-_550179.jpg/220px-DARK_CLOUDS_OF_FACTORY_SMOKE_OBSCURE_CLARK_AVENUE_BRIDGE_-_NARA_-_550179.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/DARK_CLOUDS_OF_FACTORY_SMOKE_OBSCURE_CLARK_AVENUE_BRIDGE_-_NARA_-_550179.jpg/330px-DARK_CLOUDS_OF_FACTORY_SMOKE_OBSCURE_CLARK_AVENUE_BRIDGE_-_NARA_-_550179.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/DARK_CLOUDS_OF_FACTORY_SMOKE_OBSCURE_CLARK_AVENUE_BRIDGE_-_NARA_-_550179.jpg/440px-DARK_CLOUDS_OF_FACTORY_SMOKE_OBSCURE_CLARK_AVENUE_BRIDGE_-_NARA_-_550179.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DARK_CLOUDS_OF_FACTORY_SMOKE_OBSCURE_CLARK_AVENUE_BRIDGE_-_NARA_-_550179.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Air pollution in the US, 1973</div></div></div>
<p>The burning of coal and wood, and the presence of many horses in concentrated areas made the cities the primary sources of pollution. <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">King Edward I</a> of England banned the burning of <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/seacoal" title="wikt:seacoal">sea-coal</a> by proclamation in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> in 1272, after its smoke became a problem;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pea-souper_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pea-souper-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deadly_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deadly-86">[86]</a></sup> the fuel was so common in England that this earliest of names for it was acquired because it could be carted away from some shores by the <a href="/wiki/Wheelbarrow" title="Wheelbarrow">wheelbarrow</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span></h3>
<p>It was the Industrial Revolution that gave birth to environmental pollution as we know it today. London also recorded one of the earlier extreme cases of <a href="/wiki/Water_quality" title="Water quality">water quality</a> problems with the <a href="/wiki/Great_Stink" title="Great Stink">Great Stink</a> on the <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">Thames</a> of 1858, which led to construction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London_sewerage_system" title="London sewerage system">London sewerage system</a> soon afterward. Pollution issues escalated as <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a> far exceeded viability of neighborhoods to handle their waste problem. Reformers began to demand sewer systems and clean water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1870, the sanitary conditions in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> were among the worst in Europe. <a href="/wiki/August_Bebel" title="August Bebel">August Bebel</a> recalled conditions before a modern <a href="/wiki/Sewerage" title="Sewerage">sewer</a> system was built in the late 1870s:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Waste-water from the houses collected in the gutters running alongside the curbs and emitted a truly fearsome smell. There were no public toilets in the streets or squares. Visitors, especially women, often became desperate when nature called. In the public buildings the sanitary facilities were unbelievably primitive....As a metropolis, Berlin did not emerge from a state of barbarism into civilization until after 1870.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_and_21st_century">20th and 21st century</span></h3>
<p>The primitive conditions were intolerable for a world national capital, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Germany" title="Imperial Germany">Imperial German</a> government brought in its scientists, engineers, and urban planners to not only solve the deficiencies, but to forge Berlin as the world's model city. A British expert in 1906 concluded that Berlin represented "the most complete application of science, order and method of public life," adding "it is a marvel of civic administration, the most modern and most perfectly organized city that there is."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>The emergence of great factories and consumption of immense quantities of coal gave rise to unprecedented <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a> and the large volume of industrial chemical discharges added to the growing load of untreated human waste. <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati" title="Cincinnati">Cincinnati</a> were the first two American cities to enact laws ensuring cleaner air in 1881. Pollution became a major issue in the United States in the early twentieth century, as <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Era" title="Progressive Era">progressive reformers</a> took issue with air pollution caused by coal burning, water pollution caused by bad sanitation, and street pollution caused by the three million horses who worked in American cities in 1900, generating large quantities of urine and <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a>. As historian Martin Melosi notes, the generation that first saw automobiles replacing the horses saw cars as "miracles of cleanliness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> By the 1940s, automobile-caused <a href="/wiki/Smog" title="Smog">smog</a> was a major issue in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other cities followed around the country until early in the 20th century, when the short lived Office of Air Pollution was created under the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Interior" title="United States Department of the Interior">Department of the Interior</a>. Extreme smog events were experienced by the cities of Los Angeles and <a href="/wiki/Donora,_Pennsylvania" title="Donora, Pennsylvania">Donora, Pennsylvania</a>, in the late 1940s, serving as another public reminder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donora_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Donora-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Air pollution would continue to be a problem in England, especially later during the industrial revolution, and extending into the recent past with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Smog_of_1952" title="Great Smog of 1952">Great Smog of 1952</a>. Awareness of atmospheric  pollution spread widely after World War II, with fears triggered by reports of radioactive fallout from atomic warfare and testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Then a non-nuclear event – the Great Smog of 1952 in London – killed at least 4000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> This prompted some of the first major modern environmental legislation: the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Air_Act_1956" title="Clean Air Act 1956">Clean Air Act of 1956</a>.
</p><p>
Pollution began to draw major public attention in the United States between the mid-1950s and early 1970s, when Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Noise_Control_Act" title="Noise Control Act">Noise Control Act</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Air_Act_(United_States)" title="Clean Air Act (United States)">Clean Air Act</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Water_Act" title="Clean Water Act">Clean Water Act</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/National_Environmental_Policy_Act" title="National Environmental Policy Act">National Environmental Policy Act</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-issues_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-issues-95">[95]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Air-pollution-taiwan.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Air-pollution-taiwan.JPG/220px-Air-pollution-taiwan.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Air-pollution-taiwan.JPG/330px-Air-pollution-taiwan.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Air-pollution-taiwan.JPG/440px-Air-pollution-taiwan.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Air-pollution-taiwan.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Smog pollution in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a></div></div></div>
<p>Severe incidents of pollution helped increase consciousness. <a href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_biphenyl" title="Polychlorinated biphenyl">PCB</a> dumping in the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River" title="Hudson River">Hudson River</a> resulted in a ban by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">EPA</a> on consumption of its fish in 1974. National news stories in the late 1970s – especially the long-term <a href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_dibenzodioxins" title="Polychlorinated dibenzodioxins">dioxin</a> contamination at <a href="/wiki/Love_Canal" title="Love Canal">Love Canal</a> starting in 1947 and uncontrolled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waste_disposal" title="Waste disposal">dumping</a> in <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_the_Drums" title="Valley of the Drums">Valley of the Drums</a> – led to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superfund_(environmental_law)" title="Superfund (environmental law)">Superfund</a> legislation of 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> The pollution of industrial land gave rise to the name <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brownfield" title="Brownfield">brownfield</a>, a term now common in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_planning" title="City planning">city planning</a>.
</p><p>The development of nuclear science introduced <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_contamination" title="Radioactive contamination">radioactive contamination</a>, which can remain lethally radioactive for hundreds of thousands of years. <a href="/wiki/Lake_Karachay" title="Lake Karachay">Lake Karachay</a> – named by the <a href="/wiki/Worldwatch_Institute" title="Worldwatch Institute">Worldwatch Institute</a> as the "most polluted spot" on earth – served as a disposal site for the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> throughout the 1950s and 1960s. <a href="/wiki/Ozyorsk,_Chelyabinsk_Oblast" title="Ozyorsk, Chelyabinsk Oblast">Chelyabinsk</a>, Russia, is considered the "Most polluted place on the planet".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lenssen_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lenssen-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons" title="Nuclear weapons">Nuclear weapons</a> continued to be tested in the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, especially in the earlier stages of their development. The toll on the worst-affected populations and the growth since then in understanding about the critical threat to human health posed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity">radioactivity</a> has also been a prohibitive complication associated with <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a>. Though extreme care is practiced in that industry, the potential for disaster suggested by incidents such as those at <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima</a> pose a lingering specter of public mistrust. Worldwide publicity has been intense on those disasters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Widespread support for <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_Nuclear-Test-Ban_Treaty" title="Comprehensive Nuclear-Test-Ban Treaty">test ban treaties</a> has ended almost all nuclear testing in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>International catastrophes such as the wreck of the <a href="/wiki/Amoco_Cadiz" title="Amoco Cadiz">Amoco Cadiz</a> oil tanker off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a> in 1978 and the <a href="/wiki/Bhopal_disaster" title="Bhopal disaster">Bhopal disaster</a> in 1984 have demonstrated the universality of such events and the scale on which efforts to address them needed to engage. The borderless nature of atmosphere and oceans inevitably resulted in the implication of pollution on a planetary level with the issue of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>. Most recently the term <a href="/wiki/Persistent_organic_pollutant" title="Persistent organic pollutant">persistent organic pollutant</a> (POP) has come to describe a group of chemicals such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PBDE" title="PBDE">PBDEs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fluorocarbon" title="Fluorocarbon">PFCs</a> among others. Though their effects remain somewhat less well understood owing to a lack of experimental data, they have been detected in various ecological habitats far removed from industrial activity such as the Arctic, demonstrating diffusion and <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">bioaccumulation</a> after only a relatively brief period of widespread use.
</p><p>A much more recently discovered problem is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Pacific_Garbage_Patch" title="Great Pacific Garbage Patch">Great Pacific Garbage Patch</a>, a huge concentration of plastics, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_sludge" title="Chemical sludge">chemical sludge</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Debris" title="Debris">debris</a> which has been collected into a large area of the Pacific Ocean by the <a href="/wiki/North_Pacific_Gyre" title="North Pacific Gyre">North Pacific Gyre</a>. This is a less well known pollution problem than the others described above, but nonetheless has multiple and serious consequences such as increasing wildlife mortality, the spread of invasive species and human ingestion of toxic chemicals. Organizations such as <a href="/wiki/5_Gyres" title="5 Gyres">5 Gyres</a> have researched the pollution and, along with artists like <a href="/wiki/Marina_DeBris" title="Marina DeBris">Marina DeBris</a>, are working toward publicizing the issue.
</p><p>Pollution introduced by light at night is becoming a global problem, more severe in urban centres, but nonetheless contaminating also large territories, far away from towns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Growing evidence of local and global pollution and an increasingly informed public over time have given rise to <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmentalism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_movement" title="Environmental movement">environmental movement</a>, which generally seek to limit <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">human impact on the environment</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="External_links">External links</span></h2>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="https://archive.wikiwix.com/cache/index2.php?rev_t=20110629084344&amp;url=http%3A%2Fwww.oehha.ca.gov%2Fprop65%2Fprop65_list%2FNewlist.html#federation=archive.wikiwix.com" rel="nofollow">OEHHA proposition 65 list</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20120222011524/http://ntp-server.niehs.nih.gov/" rel="nofollow">National Toxicology Program</a> – from US <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">National Institutes of Health</a>.  Reports and studies on how pollutants affect people</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.nlm.nih.gov/toxnet/index.html" rel="nofollow">TOXNET</a> – NIH databases and reports on toxicology</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.fsa.usda.gov/Assets/USDA-FSA-Public/usdafiles/Environ-Cultural/TRI%20and%20Superfund%20Environmental%20Maps%20%20TOXMAP.htm" rel="nofollow">TOXMAP</a> – Geographic Information System (GIS) that uses maps of the United States to help users visually explore data from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a> (EPA) <a href="/wiki/Toxics_Release_Inventory" title="Toxics Release Inventory">Toxics Release Inventory</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superfund_Basic_Research_Program" title="Superfund Basic Research Program">Superfund Basic Research Programs</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.epa.gov/superfund" rel="nofollow">EPA.gov</a> – manages Superfund sites and the pollutants in them (CERCLA). Map <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20101002082045/http://toxmap.nlm.nih.gov/toxmap/main/index.jsp" rel="nofollow">the EPA Superfund</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.epa.gov/toxics-release-inventory-tri-program" rel="nofollow">Toxic Release Inventory</a> – tracks how much waste US companies release into the water and air. Gives permits for releasing specific quantities of these pollutants each year.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.atsdr.cdc.gov/index.html" rel="nofollow">Agency for Toxic Substances and Disease Registry</a> – Top 20 pollutants, how they affect people, what US industries use them and the products in which they are found</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080104003923/http://www.logtv.com/films/chelyabinsk/index.htm" rel="nofollow">Chelyabinsk: The Most Contaminated Spot on the Planet</a> Documentary Film by Slawomir Grünberg (1996)</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://niemanreports.org/articles/tracking-toxics-when-the-data-are-polluted/" rel="nofollow">Nieman Reports | Tracking Toxics When the Data Are Polluted</a></li></ul>
